<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Soul</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Incorporeal essence of a living being</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Soul_(disambiguation)" title="Soul (disambiguation)">Soul (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p>In many religious and philosophical traditions, there is a belief that a <b>soul</b> is "the immaterial aspect or <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">essence</a> of a human being".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Modern English noun <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/soul" title="wikt:soul">soul</a></i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>sāwol, sāwel</i>. The earliest attestations reported in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i> are from the 8th century. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Alfred" title="King Alfred">King Alfred</a>'s translation of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Consolatione_Philosophiae" title="De Consolatione Philosophiae">De Consolatione Philosophiae</a></i>, it is used to refer to the immaterial, spiritual, or thinking aspect of a person, as contrasted with the person's physical body; in the <a href="/wiki/Vespasian_Psalter" title="Vespasian Psalter">Vespasian Psalter</a> 77.50, it means "life" or "animate existence".
</p><p>The Old English word is cognate with other historical <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> terms for the same idea, including <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>sēle, sēl</i> (which could also mean "salvation", or "solemn oath"), <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> <i>saiwala</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>sēula, sēla</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a> <i>sēola</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>sāla</i>.  Present-day cognates include Dutch <i>ziel</i> and German <i>Seele</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> and in some <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> denominations, only human beings have immortal souls (although immortality is disputed within Judaism and the concept of immortality was most likely influenced by Plato).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> For example, Thomas Aquinas, borrowing directly from Aristotle's <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Soul" title="On the Soul">On the Soul</a></i>, attributed "soul" (<i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anima" title="wikt:anima">anima</a></i>) to all organisms but argued that only human souls are immortal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Other religions (most notably <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>) believe that all living things from the smallest bacterium to the largest of mammals are the souls themselves (<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Atman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">jiva</a>) and have their physical representative (the body) in the world. The actual <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">self</a> is the soul, while the body is only a mechanism to experience the karma of that life. Thus if one sees a tiger then there is a self-conscious identity residing in it (the soul), and a physical representative (the whole body of the tiger, which is observable) in the world. Some teach that even non-biological entities (such as rivers and mountains) possess souls. This belief is called <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Near_East">Ancient Near East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient Near East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_concept_of_the_soul" title="Ancient Egyptian concept of the soul">Ancient Egyptian concept of the soul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religions_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="Religions of the ancient Near East">Religions of the ancient Near East</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2944" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg/220px-Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg/330px-Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg/440px-Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen_towing_at_Ramses%27_Temple_in_Abydos_c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Souls_of_Pe_and_Nekhen" title="Souls of Pe and Nekhen">souls of Pe and Nekhen</a> towing the <a href="/wiki/Royal_barge" title="Royal barge">royal barge</a> on a relief of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a>'s temple in Abydos.</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">ancient Egyptian religion</a>, an individual was believed to be made up of various elements, some physical and some spiritual. Similar ideas are found in ancient Assyrian and Babylonian religion. The <a href="/wiki/Kuttamuwa_stele" title="Kuttamuwa stele">Kuttamuwa stele</a>, a funeral stele for an 8th-century BCE royal official from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam%27al" title="Sam'al">Sam'al</a>, describes <a href="/wiki/Kuttamuwa" title="Kuttamuwa">Kuttamuwa</a> requesting that his mourners commemorate his life and his <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a> with feasts "for my soul that is in this stele". It is one of the earliest references to a soul as a separate entity from the body. The 800-pound (360 kg) <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> stele is 3 ft (0.91 m) tall and 2 ft (0.61 m) wide. It was uncovered in the third season of excavations by the Neubauer Expedition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago_Oriental_Institute" title="University of Chicago Oriental Institute">Oriental Institute</a> in Chicago, Illinois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bah.C3.A1.CA.BC.C3.AD_Faith"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Baháʼí_Faith">Baháʼí Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Baháʼí Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> affirms that "the soul is a sign of God, a heavenly gem whose reality the most learned of men hath failed to grasp, and whose mystery no mind, however acute, can ever hope to unravel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="Bahá'u'lláh">Bahá'u'lláh</a> stated that the soul not only continues to live after the physical death of the human body, but is, in fact, immortal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Heaven can be seen partly as the soul's state of nearness to God; and <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a> as a state of remoteness from God. Each state follows as a natural consequence of individual efforts, or the lack thereof, to develop spiritually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rob1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-rob1-9">[9]</a></sup> Bahá'u'lláh taught that individuals have no existence prior to their life here on earth and the soul's evolution is always towards God and away from the material world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rob1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-rob1-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Soul_in_the_Bible" title="Soul in the Bible">Soul in the Bible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_mortalism" title="Christian mortalism">Christian mortalism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortality_of_the_soul" title="Immortality of the soul">Immortality of the soul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_conditionalism" title="Christian conditionalism">Christian conditionalism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Annihilationism" title="Annihilationism">Annihilationism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg/220px-SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg/330px-SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg/440px-SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SoulCarriedtoHeaven.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a soul being carried to heaven by two angels by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Bouguereau" title="William Bouguereau">William Bouguereau</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to a common <a href="/wiki/Christian_eschatology" title="Christian eschatology">Christian eschatology</a>, when people die, their souls will be <a href="/wiki/Particular_judgment" title="Particular judgment">judged by God</a> and determined to go to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heaven_(Christianity)" title="Heaven (Christianity)">Heaven</a> or to <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hades" title="Christian views on Hades">Hades</a> awaiting a resurrection or the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>. The oldest branches of Christianity, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oriental_Orthodox_Churches" title="Oriental Orthodox Churches">Oriental</a> Orthodox churches, adhere to this view, as well as most Protestant denominations. A minority of Protestant Christians understand the soul as simply "life", and believe that the dead have no conscious existence until <a href="/wiki/General_judgment" title="General judgment">after the resurrection</a> (<a href="/wiki/Christian_conditionalism" title="Christian conditionalism">Christian conditionalism</a>). Some Protestant Christians believe that the souls and bodies of the unrighteous will be destroyed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hell" title="Christian views on Hell">Hell</a> rather than suffering eternally (<a href="/wiki/Annihilationism" title="Annihilationism">annihilationism</a>). Believers will inherit <a href="/wiki/Eternal_life_(Christianity)" title="Eternal life (Christianity)">eternal life</a> either in Heaven, or in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> on earth, and enjoy eternal fellowship with God.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_soul">Origin of the soul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Origin of the soul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_Damned_Soul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="606" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg/220px-Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg/330px-Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg/440px-Michelangelo%2C_Damned_Soul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelangelo,_Damned_Soul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Damned Soul</i>. Drawing by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michelangelo_Buonarroti" title="Michelangelo Buonarroti">Michelangelo Buonarroti</a> c. 1525</div></div></div>
<p>The "origin of the soul" has provided a vexing question in Christianity. The major theories put forward include <a href="/wiki/Creationism_(soul)" title="Creationism (soul)">soul creationism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traducianism" title="Traducianism">traducianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence" title="Pre-existence">pre-existence</a>. According to soul creationism, God creates each individual soul directly, either at the moment of conception or some later time. According to traducianism, the soul comes from the parents by natural generation. According to the preexistence theory, the soul exists before the moment of conception. There have been differing thoughts regarding whether human <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a> have souls from conception, or whether there is a point between conception and birth where the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> <a href="/wiki/Ensoulment" title="Ensoulment">acquires a soul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>, and/or <a href="/wiki/Personhood" title="Personhood">personhood</a>. Stances in this question might play a role in judgements on the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_abortion" title="Christianity and abortion">morality of abortion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trichotomy_of_the_soul">Trichotomy of the soul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trichotomy of the soul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a> (354-430), one of western Christianity's most influential early Christian thinkers, described the soul as "a special substance, endowed with reason, adapted to rule the body". Some Christians espouse a <a href="/wiki/Trichotomy_(philosophy)" title="Trichotomy (philosophy)">trichotomic</a> view of humans, which characterizes humans as consisting of a body (<i>soma</i>), soul (<i>psyche</i>), and spirit (<i>pneuma</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> However, the majority of modern Bible scholars point out how the concepts of "spirit" and of "soul" are used interchangeably in many biblical passages, and so hold to dichotomy: the view that each human comprises a body and a soul. Paul said that the "body wars against" the soul, "For the word of God is living and active and sharper than any two-edged sword, and piercing as far as the division of soul and spirit" (Heb 4:12 NASB), and that "I buffet my body", to keep it under control.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Views_of_various_denominations">Views of various denominations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Views of various denominations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<dl><dt>Roman Catholicism</dt></dl>
<p>The present <a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a> states that the term soul
</p>
<dl><dd>“refers to the innermost aspect of [persons], that which is of greatest value in [them], that by which [they are] most especially in God's image: ‘soul’ signifies the spiritual principle in [humanity]”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>All souls living and dead will be judged by Jesus Christ when <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">he comes back to earth</a>. The Catholic Church teaches that the existence of each individual soul is dependent wholly upon God:
</p>
<dl><dd>"The doctrine of the faith affirms that the spiritual and immortal soul is created immediately by God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG/220px-Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG/330px-Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG/440px-Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dutch_Church_Sleepy_Hollow_24.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of the soul on a 17th century tombstone at the cemetery of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch_Church_of_Sleepy_Hollow" title="Old Dutch Church of Sleepy Hollow">Old Dutch Church of Sleepy Hollow</a></div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Protestantism</dt></dl>
<p>Protestants generally believe in the soul's existence and immortality, but fall into two major camps about what this means in terms of an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>.  Some, following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Calvin" title="Jean Calvin">Jean Calvin</a>, believe that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immortality_of_the_soul" title="Immortality of the soul">soul persists</a> as consciousness after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Others, following <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, believe that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mortality_of_the_soul" title="Mortality of the soul">soul dies with the body</a>, and is unconscious ("sleeps") until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>Adventism</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p>Various <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movements</a> deriving from <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventism</a> — including <a href="/wiki/Christadelphians" title="Christadelphians">Christadelphians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Seventh-day_Adventist_Church" title="Seventh-day Adventist Church">Seventh-day Adventists</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah's Witnesses">Jehovah's Witnesses</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> — similarly believe that the dead do not possess a soul separate from the body and are unconscious until the resurrection.
</p>
<dl><dt>Latter-day Saints (‘Mormonism’)</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> teaches that the spirit and body together constitute the Soul of Man (Mankind). "The spirit and the body are the soul of man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Latter-day Saints believe that the soul is the union of a pre-existing, God-made spirit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and a temporal body, which is formed by physical conception on earth.
</p><p>After death, the spirit continues to live and progress in the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Spirit world (Latter Day Saints)">Spirit world</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>, when it is reunited with the body that once housed it. This reuniting of body and spirit results in a perfect soul that is immortal, and eternal, and capable of receiving a fulness of joy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Latter-day Saint cosmology also describes "intelligences" as the essence of consciousness or agency. These are co-eternal with God, and animate the spirits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The union of a newly-created spirit body with an eternally-existing intelligence constitutes a "spirit birth"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and justifies God's title "Father of our spirits".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confucianism">Confucianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Confucianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hun_and_po" title="Hun and po">Hun and po</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Soul_dualism" title="Soul dualism">Soul dualism</a></div>
<p>Some Confucian traditions contrast a spiritual soul with a corporeal soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hinduism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Ātman (Hinduism)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">Jiva</a></div>
<p><i>Ātman</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word that means inner <a href="/wiki/Self" title="Self">self</a> or soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidlorenzenatman_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzenatman-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedanta</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, Ātman is the <a href="/wiki/First_principle" title="First principle">first principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> the <i>true</i> self of an individual beyond identification with phenomena, the essence of an individual. In order to attain <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">liberation (moksha)</a>, a human being must acquire self-knowledge (atma <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jnana" title="Jnana">jnana</a>), which is to realize that one's true self (Ātman) is identical with the transcendent self <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a> according to <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davidlorenzenatman_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-davidlorenzenatman-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">six orthodox schools of Hinduism</a> believe that there is Ātman (self, essence) in every being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4sources_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-4sources-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, a <i>jiva</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">जीव</span>, <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">jīva</i></span>, alternative spelling <i>jiwa</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a>: <span lang="hi">जीव</span>, <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">jīv</i></span>, alternative spelling <i>jeev</i>) is a living being, or any entity imbued with a life force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of <i>jiva</i> in Jainism is similar to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atman_(Hinduism)" title="Atman (Hinduism)">atman</a></i> in Hinduism. However, some Hindu traditions differentiate between the two concepts, with <i>jiva</i> considered as individual self, while atman as that which is universal unchanging self that is present in all living beings and everything else as the metaphysical <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varenne46_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-varenne46-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The latter is sometimes referred to as <i>jiva-atman</i> (a soul in a living body).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-varenne46_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-varenne46-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/R%C5%AB%E1%B8%A5" title="Rūḥ">Rūḥ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nafs" title="Nafs">Nafs</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, the holy book of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, uses two words to refer to the soul: <i><a href="/wiki/R%C5%AB%E1%B8%A5" title="Rūḥ">rūḥ</a></i> (translated as spirit, consciousness, pneuma or "soul") and <i><a href="/wiki/Nafs" title="Nafs">nafs</a></i> (translated as self, ego, psyche or "soul"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talib_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talib-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brag_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-brag-46">[46]</a></sup> cognates of the Hebrew <i>nefesh</i> and <i>ruach</i>. The two terms are frequently used interchangeably, though <i>rūḥ</i> is more often used to denote the divine spirit or "the breath of life", while <i>nafs</i> designates one's disposition or characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In Islamic philosophy, the immortal rūḥ "drives" the mortal nafs, which comprises temporal desires and perceptions necessary for living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmad_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmad-48">[48]</a></sup>
Two of the passages in the Quran that mention the rûh occur in chapters 17 ("The Night Journey") and 39 ("The Troops"):
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And they ask you, [O Muhammad], about the Rûh. Say, "The Rûh is of the affair of my Lord. And mankind has not been given of knowledge except a little.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_17:85" title="Quran 17:85">Quran 17:85</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Allah takes the souls at the time of their death, and those that do not die [He takes] during their sleep. Then He keeps those for which He has decreed death and releases the others for a specified term. Indeed in that are signs for a people who give thought..</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_39:42" title="Quran 39:42">Quran 39:42</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Jainism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/J%C4%ABva_(Jainism)" title="Jīva (Jainism)">Jīva (Jainism)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitalism_(Jainism)" title="Vitalism (Jainism)">Vitalism (Jainism)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Jain_philosophy" title="Jain philosophy">Jain philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism_and_non-creationism" title="Jainism and non-creationism">Jainism and non-creationism</a></div>
<p>In Jainism, every living being, from plant or bacterium to human, has a soul and the concept forms the very basis of Jainism. According to Jainism, there is no beginning or end to the existence of soul. It is eternal in nature and changes its form until it attains liberation.
</p><p>In Jainism, <i>jiva</i> is the immortal essence or soul of a living organism (human, animal, fish or plant etc.) which survives physical death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jajinixxii_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-jajinixxii-49">[49]</a></sup> The concept of <i>Ajiva</i> in Jainism means "not soul", and represents matter (including body), time, space, non-motion and motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jajinixxii_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-jajinixxii-49">[49]</a></sup> In Jainism, a <i>Jiva</i> is either <i>samsari</i> (mundane, caught in cycle of rebirths) or <i>mukta</i> (liberated).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to this belief until the time the soul is liberated from the <i><a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra_(Jainism)" title="Saṃsāra (Jainism)">saṃsāra</a></i> (cycle of repeated birth and death), it gets attached to one of these bodies based on the <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Jainism" title="Karma in Jainism">karma</a> (actions) of the individual soul. Irrespective of which state the soul is in, it has got the same attributes and qualities. The difference between the liberated and non-liberated souls is that the qualities and attributes are manifested completely in case of <i><a href="/wiki/Siddha" title="Siddha">siddha</a></i> (liberated soul) as they have overcome all the karmic bondages whereas in case of non-liberated souls they are partially exhibited. Souls who rise victorious over wicked emotions while still remaining within physical bodies are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Arihant_(Jainism)" title="Arihant (Jainism)">arihants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Concerning the Jain view of the soul, <a href="/wiki/Virchand_Gandhi" title="Virchand Gandhi">Virchand Gandhi</a> said
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the soul lives its own life, not for the purpose of the body, but the body lives for the purpose of the soul. If we believe that the soul is to be controlled by the body then soul misses its power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soul&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Primary_sources plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Primary_sources" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>relies excessively on <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">references</a> to <a
